```
 
That is a very narrow and pedantic definition, Howard.

I agree it is technically accurate, but only in the exact sense of the 
words.

People who support crime, who endorse criminal values, who see a life of 
crime as romantic and bohemian, who sell goods they never stole themselves 
(but know full well to have been stolen) in pub bars and car boot sales, who 
think there is "street cred" in fraternising with and taking favours from, 
criminals, while they may never have committed a crime technically, are 
still, to my wayof thinking, much more culpable (and "criminal") than the 
guy who parks too long on a meter, jaywalks a crossing, or is found to be 
drunk and disorderly.

In all of my posts, I have used the term "criminal" in the wider, rather 
than narrower sense.

Even in the narrower sense, we still can't know how many "criminals" are out 
there because many crimes are not reported, for various reasons.

Pete.

TOP POST nothing new below.

```

"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:1136349782.125618.89930@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com...
>
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Sometimes, if the word has a broader meaning, yes. Doesn't make it wrong, 
but it changes the argument...

>> People who support crime, who endorse criminal values, who see a life of
>> crime as romantic and bohemian, who sell goods they never stole 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> criminals even when the word is used 'narrowly and pedantically'.

Sure, I never argued that... But there are people who sell goods without 
technically "receiving" them in the legal sense of the word. Are they 
"criminals"? Not technically, in the strict meaning of the word, but I 
believe they are... My point (broadly) is that, while commiting a crime 
certainly makes one a criminal (in any sense of the word), there are people 
who don't necessarily commit crimes, but are part of a criminal community. I 
believe "punishment" should be designed to get people to realise that the 
negative outcomes from crime are bad for all of us and, if they are part of 
the criminal community but not yet technically criminal, encourage them to 
not go the next step. I further believe that  a softer, sensible approach is 
more likely to have this effect than a hard line God of Vengeance approach 
with "punishments" that demean the person who has to administer them, the 
perpetrator who is alienated by them, and all of the rest of us, who, as a 
community, condone these pointless penalties.

>> who
>> think there is "street cred" in fraternising with and taking favours 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>
You should know me better than that, Richard. It isn't a crime (and 
hopefully never will be) to THINK about ANYTHING.

Alternatives must be weighed and subjective decisions reached.  I'm not 
talking about people who think about crime; I'm talking about people who 
condone the criminal "way of life" and see it as a winning thing. People who 
think the general public are "mugs" and "fair game". I believe these people 
are just as much a problem for society as the criminals who have already 
broken the law. The difference is that these people are "innocent" under the 
law, even though they may not be in actuality. I'm not suggesting we arrest 
people for holding a point of view :-); I am suggesting we target such 
people with a view to changing their minds...

Social engineering in this day and age is achievable. Look at the drink and 
drive campaigns here in NZ that have caused a dramatic drop in the road 
toll... Blanket TV campaigns with slogans like "They drink; we die", "If you 
drink and drive, you're a bloody idiot" etc. After living in Germany for a 
number of years (where the idea of drinking and driving has been considered 
socially unacceptable for at least 25 years to my knowledge, and nobody  (i 
know) does it...) and then returning to NZ where it was considered a bit of 
a game to beat the cops and "everyone knew" that it was really OK to drink 
and drive as long as you only had a few... I have been agreeably surprised 
to find more and more people of all ages simply not doing it. Their minds 
have been changed. The result is a diminishing road toll, despite traffice 
increasing dramatically (around 10% per year).  Interestingly (and very 
sadly) this holiday period bucked the trend:
2000 = 20,
2001 = 21
2002 = 17
2003 = 13
2004 = 11
2005 = 20

Despite the increased holiday toll, the overall road deaths this year (404) 
was the lowest for 42 years. The campaigns are working. For statistical 
breakdowns over 5 years, check this link: 
http://www.ltsa.govt.nz/research/toll.html

It can be done. So far it hasn't been tried with crime, but I see no reason 
why it couldn't be.

>> guy who parks too long on a meter, jaywalks a crossing, or is found to be
>> drunk and disorderly.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Well, if you relate it to countries where this IS a crime, my argument 
stands.

Pete.
```

Pete Dashwood wrote:

> > Many of which are _not_ crimes (depending od country), thus the
> > infringers are _not_ criminals.
> >
> Well, if you relate it to countries where this IS a crime, my argument
> stands.

You miss the point, the law is divided up into Civil Law, Criminal Law
and Torts. Jaywalking and parking infringements are against the law,
but are only Torts and are not part of _Criminal_ Law, thus are not
'crimes'.

Or is that too narrow, pedantic and accurate for you ?

```

"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:4222h1F1gl0kqU1@individual.net...

> I believe "punishment" should be designed to get people to realise that 
> the negative outcomes from crime are bad for all of us and, if they are 
> part of the criminal community but not yet technically criminal, encourage 
> them to not go the next step.

Getting people to realize that it is important not to do anything that's bad 
*for others* -- criminal or not -- is not so easy.  There are a *great* many 
people out there who are so egocentric that they are fundamentally unable to 
notice or to care that an action might deleteriously affect someone else. 
*Informing* someone that such an act is fundamentally harmful to all of us 
is a far cry from ensuring that they *learn* that that is the case, 
particularly when the actor is convinced that their actions are justifiable 
or reasonable under the circumstances.

     -Chuck Stevens
```

"Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote in message 
news:dph62s$29nb$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com...
>
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> that their actions are justifiable or reasonable under the circumstances.

Yes, I completely agree. I used the word "persuade"; simply telling people 
is not enough. We have an industry designed to "persuade" people to buy 
stuff they neither need nor want. It is very effective. So this leads me to 
believe that people can be persuaded :-)

It needs to be a many-pronged attack starting in early school. And I see 
penalties for lawbreaking as being part of it. We have abolished the cat of 
nine tails and thumbscrews and hot irons.... let's simply continue this 
growth path and make penalties meaningful instead of pointless.

If there were penalties that fit the crime and had a positive outcome, I 
believe some minds could be changed. It seems to work here in places where 
we have done it.  Letting perpetrators see the full effect of their actions 
and the pain caused, not just to the victim, can have an effect. (It won't 
always, but neither does locking them up...) Responsibility and genuine 
remorse with compensation to the victim by the perpetrator, and constructive 
work to "pay off the debt" to society has to be a better form of 
"punishment" than simple deprivation of freedom or breaking rocks. 
Obviously, there are some crimes where this can't be done and I covered that 
in an earlier post.

That is the nub of my argument here.

Pete.
```

On 3 Jan 2006 20:43:02 -0800, "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>> guy who parks too long on a meter, jaywalks a crossing, or is found to be
>> drunk and disorderly.
>
>Many of which are _not_ crimes (depending od country), thus the
>infringers are _not_ criminals.

I don't like the "broader" definition.   It is too much like the way
the Religious Right defines sins that are not well defined in the
Scriptures.   Stuff that they don't like are just "wrong". 

Some conservative values are like this.   My music is right, my kids
music is wrong.   That person speaking Spanish is a criminal wetback.
Smoking is criminal.   Homosexuals will go to Hell.

Some liberal values are like this as well, wanting to punish thought
crimes.

If we step beyond what the law (or the scriptures) actually say and
allow us to interpret crime and sin to our tastes, we are in dangerous
territory.

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message 
news:t4iar1h0972vdgvndsqlaqak7l4hd5utng@4ax.com...
>
> If someone's profession is crime, he continues committing crimes.
> Rolling the dice, his number will come up eventually.   Even for the
> perfect professional (in programming or crime) - screwups happen.

    Assuming that a "criminal" is "someone who commits at least one crime", 
then there might exist a person who originally had no intent in ever 
becoming a criminal (i.e. has a regular day job), but then finds himself in 
a situation where he can commit a crime and be virtually guaranteed not to 
get caught, with a tremendous payoff. He mulls over the probabilities for a 
few days, and weighs the benefits versus the drawbacks, and eventually 
decides to do it. And never gets caught. And never commits a crime again.

    - Oliver

```
On Mon, 09 Jan 2006 15:34:17 GMT, "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com>
wrote:

>> If someone's profession is crime, he continues committing crimes.
>> Rolling the dice, his number will come up eventually.   Even for the
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>decides to do it. And never gets caught. And never commits a crime again.

Sure.   I suspect a fair percentage of murders are like this.   But
there's no way that most criminals stop with one.   

I used the term "professional criminal", but we can expand this.   How
many speeders speed only once?   How many politicians accept exactly
one bribe?    How many abusers or rapists stop with one atrocity?

When you get away with something, the tendency is to keep going.    It
is my contention that most criminals keep committing crimes.

And it is my contention that when those who keep committing crimes
find that screw-ups happen.

I read a John Dortmunder story where this fictional thief was hired to
advice a rich man on how to steal a sculpture that that man's wife had
as part of her divorce settlement.   Dortmunder vetoed one of the rich
man's ideas because it involved breaking the law (flying a helicopter
to her house in Manhattan).    His rule was - only break the one law
you intend to break - people get arrested for double parking when they
meant to rob a bank.     Westlake is funny, but his point is valid -
stuff happens.

It certainly seems that way sometimes. Generations of criminals (I became 
acquainted once with a professional pickpocket who was very proud of the 
fact that he was continuing a fine family tradition, now in its sixth 
generation, with skills and training being passed to each child) certainly 
exist, and many of them have never been caught. I don't know what the answer 
is in cases like that. I don't know how you persuade someone who has been 
exposed to that as a way of life, all their life, to go and get a job... :-)

You may have noticed that in several of my posts recently I have advocated 
that we stop relying/waiting/depending on God to sort the world out (which 
generally means salvation for the faithful and torture for the rest of us; 
not really much of a "sort out" in my opinion), and, instead, start taking 
some personal responsibility for ourselves and our communities. This is 
something that each of us can do, even if in a small way. We need to help 
the needy, not the greedy, and we need to support and encourage each other 
to grow. Parents need to show their kids (ideally by example) that they are 
part of a bigger picture and have a responsibility not just to their family 
and their community, but to all men (actually to all life forms...), so 
don't drop litter, don't vandalize, don't step on bugs or snakes, don't 
bully or abuse other kids, etc.

Pete.

I don't want to answer for Donald (who is more than capable of answering for 
himself :-)), but my take on this is that a "non-barbaric" system, by 
seeking to actually persuade people against crime and change their minds 
about the desirability of committing it (not just by threatening fearful 
punishments, but by actually addressing the root causes and helping 
individuals to deal with them), has at least a fighting chance of breaking 
the inherited cycle of bad behaviour. Sometimes people can be persuaded that 
what their fathers did may no longer be optimal. It can be done without 
denigrating or diminishing their fathers (different strokes for different 
times) but the fact is that at THIS place, in THIS time, it probably isn't a 
good thing to rape and pillage unsuspecting villages... :-) I'm sure you 
catch my drift... :-)

Pete.

```

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message 
news:apmnr1pnj0k9dbmgs6mel4kd7an2vv71h0@4ax.com...
> On Wed, 4 Jan 2006 16:06:37 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Whatever we come up with (including my own ideas on this) is unlikely to be 
a 100% solution. It requires a fundamental approach at an early age and it 
has to be "many-pronged". Family, schools, the community as a whole, needs 
to foster a desire for positive outcomes that improve things for everybody. 
People need to feel part of their community and not isolated by or from it. 
It's a big ask. It can't happen overnight but the starfish approach is 
pertinent here.

(Two men are walking along a beach after a recent storm. There are thousands 
of starfish washed up on the beach. One man starts picking them up and 
throwing them back to the water. The other man says: "There's no point. 
There are thousands of them. We couldn't possibly save them all. Whatever we 
do can't make a difference."
The first man replies: "It made a difference to the ones I threw back.")

Every kid who accepts responsibility for their crime, does what they can to 
make it right, does community service or benefits by helping old folk or 
working with kids, and has his/her mind changed about a life of crime, is a 
success that eventually will filter through to society as a whole.

There will always be criminals. The reasons that cause people to embark on 
such a life are complex and manifold. As a society at large, we can show 
more about our own maturity and understanding by the penalties we impose on 
lawbreakers, and this, in turn, has an effect on the community. It's a bit 
like parenting. Kids follow the role models. If you live in a narrow minded, 
vengeful society, you will tend to be narrow minded and vengeful. 
Fortunately, the converse is also true.

Pete.
```

> But which of these two people is the bigger danger to you and yours?

Depends on what the passerby did to set him off.

I'm inclined to be sympathetic if said passers-by were weaving in and 
out of lanes while gabbing on cell phones.
```

```
Top post, no more below.

On the model of auris (ear), ignis (fire), orbis (circle), and tussis 
(cough), it appears (based on the public domain "A Latin Grammar" by Charles 
E. Bennett, which tome is available for browsing and for download from 
http://www.textkit,com ) that the regular nominative plural of the Latin 
word "penis" (tail) is, according to the rules for the Third Declension, 
"penes".

    -Chuck Stevens

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message 
news:72m2r15tql1e0unqk6n67d0ojjobaud3v4@4ax.com...

> <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca> wrote:

> I do like alternative plurals - such as "subschemata".

```
Top post, no more below:

I'm neither a particular fan nor a particular enemy of capital punishment.

I am however of the opinion that, from the standpoint of actions, anybody 
can do whatever they wish to do *so long as* they are willing to pay the 
price.

If a given society has determined that the price for a given action is to be 
drawn and quartered, it is inappropriate for the perpetrator of that action 
to expect any consequence other than being drawn and quartered.

If a given society has determined that the price of a given action is to be 
slapped on the hand and told "Naughty!  Naughy!", it is appropriate for the 
perpetrator of that action to expect nothing more unpleasant than being 
slapped on the hand and told "Naughty!  Naughty!".

I don't think of criminal sentences as being punishments so much as 
consequences that [a given] society has determined are appropriate to a 
given action.   In an ideal world there would be a one-to-one correspondence 
between the action and its predetermined consequence.

And as far as I'm concerned that's entirely appropriate regardless of the 
perpetrator's subsequent rehabilitation.  You do the crime, you do the time. 
If you don't want the consequences, don't take the action in the first 
place.

    -Chuck Stevens


"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message

```
On Tue, 3 Jan 2006 08:36:51 -0800, "Chuck Stevens"
<charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote:

>I am however of the opinion that, from the standpoint of actions, anybody 
>can do whatever they wish to do *so long as* they are willing to pay the 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>to expect any consequence other than being drawn and quartered.

IMO, the worst aspect of capital punishment is when it is used as a
political tool.   Often times there are two types of people being
punished by the state - those who have been found guilty of committing
crimes, and those that have been found to be enemies of the state.   

The enemies of the state fall into two categories - those who are
guilty of crimes (maybe civil disobedience), and those who haven't.
Certainly the latter category don't fit your description.

The other area of concern is when innocent people get convicted of
crimes.   I'm not one of those who believe life in prison is more
humane than execution.    But there have been times when minor crimes
have had major punishments.     While it might be inappropriate for a
perpetrator to expect to avoid that punishment - it is extremely
appropriate for an innocent bystander to expect to avoid that
punishment.
```

>
> I'm neither a particular fan nor a particular enemy of capital punishment.

Me neither. I do think death is pretty drastic as "punishment", but there 
are some crimes that demand elimination of the perpetrator from civilised 
society. Exile doesn't really work because you are just unloading your scum 
onto other countries, so death is probably the most expedient option. I 
s'pose you could see if any other country would take them on bleeding heart 
or religious grounds, but, if they drew a blank it has to be the little 
needle...

> I am however of the opinion that, from the standpoint of actions, anybody 
> can do whatever they wish to do *so long as* they are willing to pay the 
> price.

Hmmm... not sure about "whatever they wish"... some imaginative people might 
dream up crimes that it would be impossible to set a suitable punishment 
for... Things so wicked that death (even a slow lingering tortured one) 
could not begin to balance the ledger (actually, this is one of the reasons 
I don't believe that "punishment" balances the crime, although I agree that 
it should, where this can be done feasibly and with a positive outcome)... 
"whatever they wish" is fine for sane people, but perhaps not for the 
criminally deranged. Anyway, leaving this reservation aside for the moment, 
I see what you are saying, and do not disagree.

>
> If a given society has determined that the price for a given action is to 
> be drawn and quartered, it is inappropriate for the perpetrator of that 
> action to expect any consequence other than being drawn and quartered.

drawing and quartering...

> If a given society has determined that the price of a given action is to

> the perpetrator of that action to expect nothing more unpleasant than 
> being slapped on the hand and told "Naughty!  Naughty!".

Quite so. However, I would personally argue for something more positive than 
being slapped on the hand and told "Naughty!  Naughty!".


> I don't think of criminal sentences as being punishments so much as 
> consequences that [a given] society has determined are appropriate to a 
> given action.
Yes. The problem is that very often the "consequences" are required by the 
society to be much more negative than is really necessary.

> In an ideal world there would be a one-to-one correspondence between the 
> action and its predetermined consequence.
>
In MY ideal world there would be a one-to-one correspondence between the 
action and its predetermined consequence, with the emphasis of the 
consequence being positive, and intended to persuade that a repeat of the 
offence (or any other offence, for that matter...) was definitely a bad 
idea....

> And as far as I'm concerned that's entirely appropriate regardless of the 
> perpetrator's subsequent rehabilitation.  You do the crime, you do the 
> time. If you don't want the consequences, don't take the action in the 
> first place.
>
I agree with your principle, it is only the means of consequence that we 
would need to discuss.
<snip>

```

"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:420uahF1g3qi9U1@individual.net...

> I agree with your principle, it is only the means of consequence that we 
> would need to discuss.

One of the issues that remains open is the meaning of "I would personally 
argue for something more positive ...".  What is "positive" and what is 
"negative" is in the eye of the beholder, whereas what is *effective* may 
depend more on the mindset of the particular offender than anything else.  A 
great many offenders are unwilling or unable to accept responsibility for 
their actions, and part of the issue here is society's insistence that yes, 
indeed, we *are* responsible for their actions.

Examples of this can be drawn from cases as extreme and diverse as the "evil 
government" approach from Timothy McVeigh, the Twinkie Defense (an urban 
legend, as it turns out) of Dan White, and "the devil made me do it" made 
famous by Geraldine Jones (AKA Flip Wilson).  Point being, offenders 
frequently feel their actions can be justified, or that they are powerless 
to avoid the actions in the first place, in which case no amount of 
persuasion will convince them otherwise.

I've thought a good deal about the McVeigh case.  And the conclusion I came 
to is that, presuming he was legally both *sane* and *not sociopathic* at 
the time of the crime the *appropriate sane* response to his actions would 
be to commit suicide.   The fact that he could *not* bring himself to accept 
responsibility for the horrors he wrought and instead argued for the bombing 
of the Murrah building as an *appropriate and reasonable* response to the 
government's actions against the Branch Davidians in Waco, argues 
persuasively for his *sanity*.   I don't believe a sane, nonsociopathic 
person could truly face having murdered 160 people without either blaming 
someone or something else for the act, or committing suicide.

Stated rather more briefly, the person who uses "Yeah, but ..." a great deal 
is not likely to be convinced by "nevertheless, ...".

    -Chuck Stevens
```

law that the actions they take in the name of their religion are illegal is 
to deny them *their* right to practice their religion as they understand it, 
or even as they have been taught it.   In such a case, "reeducation" and 
"rehabilitation" themselves can, I think realistically, be shown to be 
violations of the perpetrator's First Amendment rights to free exercize of 
his religion!

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
On Thu, 5 Jan 2006 09:14:41 -0800, "Chuck Stevens"
<charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote:

>Which is exactly my point.  To try to convince such a person as a point of 
>law that the actions they take in the name of their religion are illegal is 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>his religion!

Certainly the novel _1984_ gives an example of reeducation that has
been shown to be seen as the ideal for many totalitarian states.

One of the arguments for negative income tax is as follows:

Everybody gets this "welfare".   Most of us don't bother to pick up
our check, preferring to simply use it as our standard personal
deduction from our income tax.

But since everybody gets it - nobody is a "welfare mom".    Why is
this important?   It is important because "my mom is smart and good",
therefore what she does is smart and good, and her identity is mine.
Figuring out how to get money from the state is smart and good.

If everybody gets that paycheck - then my Mom works during the busy
hours on Friday and Saturday nights at the local restaurant (why not,
it doesn't disqualify her from any welfare, and every little bit
helps?).   Now instead of being a welfare mom, she's a waitress. Now,
instead of having an identity of someone who knows how to use the
system, our identity is of being workers within the system.
```

```
 

"Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote in message 
news:dpjk62$n16$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com...
>
>
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
>

It is dilemmas such as this which led originally to the need for separation 
of Church and State. If your religion requires you to strangle chickens 
publicly in the local Mall at noon every second Thursday, or to eviscerate 
your neighbour's eldest child when the Planet Mars is in Perigee, there is 
likely to be some resistance from the community at large.

Preventing you from doing unacceptable acts, but still allowing you to 
practice your religion freely, is the responsibility of the State. For 
example, the State may require you to do your chicken strangling in your own 
back yard or ensure that it is done somewhere where other people cannot see 
it and be offended by it. It might be suggested that the practice of child 
evisceration be reconsidered and if not altogether abolished, perhaps a goat 
or lamb could be substituted...(The Indian government have exactly this 
problem in trying to stamp out the practise of suttee, and the caste 
system).

For all of these reasons, it makes sense for the secular authority to be 
superior to (or have authority over) the religious one. Religious fanatics 
who argue for religious government will find it is only achievable in places 
where the majority of the population ascribe to the particular religion. In 
diverse populations it can never work, and even in populations where 
everybody agrees with the religion, it hinders the growth of the society and 
limits it to a status quo that can last for centuries.

The sooner the world grows out of the need for religion, the better it will 
be for all of us. Having said that, I don't expect it to happen anytime in 
the next couple of thousand years... :-)

I am thankful that I live in a free (secularly governed) society where 
holding such a view does not make me subject to burning at the stake, or 
having hot instruments applied to my body. (Some progress has been made in 
the last couple of thousand years... :-))

Pete.

>    -Chuck Stevens
>
>
```

"Alistair" <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote in message 
news:1135375616.234551.254840@g43g2000cwa.googlegroups.com...
>
> Oliver Wong wrote:
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> the remake. Maybe they'll have the Mummy returning for the third time?

    One issue with remakes is that people (including movie makers) generally 
only remember the classics, or "good" movies, and so when they want to do a 
remake, it'll probably be one of those movies that will get remade. If they 
took a really lousy movie, and did a remake of it, it'd be a lot easier for 
the remake to be better than the original, but there'd be less incentive to 
advertise what movie this new one is a remake of.

    This isn't a movie, but "Chrono Trigger" was one of my favorite video 
games. It was released in 1995 for the Super Nintendo console. The plot of 
the game, I thought was excellent, and the graphics were decent for that 
age. Screenshot at:
http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/d5/ChronoTriggerInside.PNG

    Some fans of CT got together and decided to make a remake of the game. 
As far as I know, it would have the exact same plot, but the graphics, music 
and some of the gameplay would be revised. Screenshot at: 
http://www.opcoder.com/projects/chrono/Imps.jpg

    There's a trailer also available for the remake, and it was quite an 
emotional experience for me watching it. The music and scenes invoked a lot 
of memories. What I was basically thinking is that I could re-enjoy Chrono 
Trigger again, and in all aspects, it would be equal or better. 
Unfortunately, the project got cancelled for legal reasons (namely they 
didn't acquire the rights to the license).

    - Oliver
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT: Remakes of movies

_(thread depth: 62)_

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2006-01-09T09:56:05-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<cn35s1134iv732rmeh3tqtt4q6piht9ngl@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<9rjqf.16236$kP5.14258@bignews5.bellsouth.net> <cnhjq1lj6emfqktk3t26tkfr1eb762vsgt@4ax.com> <84kqf.1196$km.1136@edtnps89> <40ud00F1c6ob8U1@individual.net> <ckBqf.26483$k76.2648@bignews6.bellsouth.net> <84plq1prbt7r1cerdgfuinse4arr83fqpv@4ax.com> <boCqf.169704$Gd6.47105@pd7tw3no> <1135285228.000852.147670@g49g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <CLFqf.333$OU5.206@clgrps13> <1135375616.234551.254840@g43g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <hHvwf.46735$km.40115@edtnps89>`

```
> I can not contemplate the notion that Casblanca would be any better on
> the remake. Maybe they'll have the Mummy returning for the third time?

Occasionally the remake is better than the original.   How many people
have seen the 1931 version of _The Maltese Falcon_?    I haven't. But
the thing is the 1931 version is the original *movie*, not the
Dashiell Hammett novel.

Popular culture being what it is, when a work is remade a lot, origins
get confused.   The makers of _The Adams Family_ movies used a
character from the TV show, and ended up paying royalties to those who
owned the TV series (they claimed they were basing the movie directly
off the Charles Adams comics in The New Yorker magazine).   

The movie _Little Shop of Horrors_ was based off the play which was
based off the movie _The Little Shop of Horrors_.

We have other non-musical -> musical examples.   The movie _My Fair
Lady_ wasn't really a remake of the movie _Pygmalian_, but instead was
a filming of the play which was the remake.    Cole Porter turned
_Philadelphia Story_ into _High Society_ with his songs.   These
examples are good movies remade into good movies.

We are CoBOL programmers.   We remake programs.   We copy code.   We
plagiarize code.    Because we have the same story to tell again and
again - with our environments changed just enough so that the old way
doesn't quite work.

There's a SF story (by Spider Robinson?) where in the future computers
check every song that ever created for plagiarism.   A rich widow of a
songwriter lobbies to make this less inclusive after her husband,
trying to write her another love song kept failing to come up with
something that wasn't derivative committed suicide.   They needed to
recycle creativity.

We don't have patents on steering wheels or foot throttles.   We don't
have patents on shooting up monsters in the arcade.

Patents have been very good for society for some time now, but are
possibly coming to the end of their useful life cycle.    We don't
seem to get innovation via big companies' patents anymore - and I
don't think we are getting the products from the small guys via
patents so much either.
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<lzxwf.80029$6K2.49736@edtnps90>`
- **References:** `<9rjqf.16236$kP5.14258@bignews5.bellsouth.net> <cnhjq1lj6emfqktk3t26tkfr1eb762vsgt@4ax.com> <84kqf.1196$km.1136@edtnps89> <40ud00F1c6ob8U1@individual.net> <ckBqf.26483$k76.2648@bignews6.bellsouth.net> <84plq1prbt7r1cerdgfuinse4arr83fqpv@4ax.com> <boCqf.169704$Gd6.47105@pd7tw3no> <1135285228.000852.147670@g49g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <CLFqf.333$OU5.206@clgrps13> <1135375616.234551.254840@g43g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <hHvwf.46735$km.40115@edtnps89> <cn35s1134iv732rmeh3tqtt4q6piht9ngl@4ax.com>`

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message 
news:cn35s1134iv732rmeh3tqtt4q6piht9ngl@4ax.com...
>
> We don't have patents on steering wheels or foot throttles.   We don't
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> patents so much either.

    I believe that the original intent of patents was to promote innovation. 
That is, previously, people didn't bother to think up ideas, because it 
wasn't profitable. People could just take the ideas and build their own 
products based on the idea. So patents were created so that the person who 
came up with the idea was allowed a monopoly on the idea for a few years, 
giving him/her a chance to make some profit off of the idea.

    Nowadays, patents are stiffling innovation. People are too afraid to 
create new products 'cause it might infringe on some big company's patent. 
Big companies are not patenting everything because it's too dangerous not 
to. Even altruistic companies need to do this: If I were head of a "good" 
software company, and had the opportunity to patent the concept of using the 
mouse and keyboard to interact with a computer, I definitely would; not 
because I plan then on suing everyone else who infringed on my patent, but 
because I don't want to risk allowing my competitor to get this patent and 
then sue me!

    - Oliver
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT: Remakes of movies

_(thread depth: 64)_

- **From:** mwojcik@newsguy.com (Michael Wojcik)
- **Date:** 2006-01-10T18:51:38+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<dq0vnq01ua7@news4.newsguy.com>`
- **References:** `<9rjqf.16236$kP5.14258@bignews5.bellsouth.net> <84kqf.1196$km.1136@edtnps89> <40ud00F1c6ob8U1@individual.net> <ckBqf.26483$k76.2648@bignews6.bellsouth.net> <84plq1prbt7r1cerdgfuinse4arr83fqpv@4ax.com> <boCqf.169704$Gd6.47105@pd7tw3no> <1135285228.000852.147670@g49g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <CLFqf.333$OU5.206@clgrps13> <1135375616.234551.254840@g43g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <hHvwf.46735$km.40115@edtnps89> <cn35s1134iv732rmeh3tqtt4q6piht9ngl@4ax.com> <lzxwf.80029$6K2.49736@edtnps90>`

```

In article <lzxwf.80029$6K2.49736@edtnps90>, "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com> writes:
> 
>     I believe that the original intent of patents was to promote innovation. 

The original intent of patents - from "letters patent", ie letters
published to the general public - in this sense (as opposed to
land-grant patents, letters patent conveying a title, and so forth)
was to reward people for service to the crown by giving them an
artificial monopoly.  No innovation was required.  It wasn't long
before governments started issuing letters patent granting monopolies
in order to make risky enterprises, notably overseas trade, economi-
cally viable, and so created the Dutch East India Company and the
like.  Again, the people receiving those patents weren't being
rewarded for being clever - ruthless is more like it.

> That is, previously, people didn't bother to think up ideas, because it 
> wasn't profitable.

This was a joke, right?

> People could just take the ideas and build their own 
> products based on the idea.

Not when artificial-monopoly patents were introduced they couldn't.
This was feudal Europe at the beginning of the Early Modern period;
mercantilism and the entrepreneurial class were just starting to
appear.  Sure, there were coffeeshops (a harbinger of the rise of the
middle class in Europe; see Stallybrass and White's _The Politics and
Poetics of Transgression_) starting to appear in the most cosmopoli-
tan cities, but the idea of commercial competition was still mostly
just a gleam in a clerk's eye.

Very few people were in any position to take an idea and profit from
it, at least by providing goods or services.

> So patents were created so that the person who 
> came up with the idea was allowed a monopoly on the idea for a few years, 
> giving him/her a chance to make some profit off of the idea.

Intellectual-property patents apparently first came on the scene (in
Europe at least) in Venice, but letters patent granting monopolies had
already been around for some time.  In English law, the intellectual-
property patent wasn't the dominant type until James I, and there was
plenty of innovation before then.

> Nowadays, patents are stiffling innovation.

That's a popular opinion, and like most popular opinions, it's a
gross generalization.  There is no clear evidence or compelling
argument to show that innovation would be significantly greater were
patents suddenly overturned or significantly restricted.  While it's
difficult to argue that patent-granting authorities such as the USPTO
don't grant numerous foolish patents (and they're certainly over-
whelmed by volume, political pressure, and lobbying), it's not
immediately clear how to fix that without making the patent-granting
process even more difficult and expensive - which raises the barrier
to entry even higher for individuals and small businesses.

Intellectual property law is a mess.  But it's always been a mess
(when it's existed at all, and only the very naive think things would
be better in a state of nature).  It has of late undergone significant
and useful change, and it remains in flux, so at least there's hope
for progress.  Which is more than you can say of many problems.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Remakes of movies

_(thread depth: 62)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2006-01-10T23:51:12+13:00

    Videogame remakes are popular for hobbyist programmers, because they can 
just concentrate on the programming. The graphics, music, story, etc. can 
all be lifted from the original game.

    A Google search for "space invader remake" (without the quotes) yields 
about 50'000 hits, the first 10 at least are all to sites that talk about a 
space invader remake, possibly offering one for download.

    Going to sourceforge.net and searching for "remake", of the first 25 
results, 24 are remakes of games. One of them is some sort of command like 
interpreter which happens to have the word "remake" show up in its 
description.

```

 Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net> wrote:

> On Tue, 27 Dec 2005 09:49:29 -0500, "charles hottel"
> <jghottel@yahoo.com> wrote:
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> itself".

I'm a late arrival to this thread, but I will perhaps make an attempt.

There are two possible universe styles the "open" and the "closed".

The idea of the "closed" universe is such that the gravity of the 
universe pulls the expanding edges back into itself.  This configuration 
will eventually collapse upon itself.

The idea of the "open" universe is that the gravity generated by the 
mass of the universe is not enough to pull the entire thing back in upon 
itself.  Imagine picking up a pancake by placing a finger in the center.  
The sides of the pancake would drape down and form a saddle shape -- 
this is the "open" universe.  It will continue to expand, but its own 
gravity will distort its shape.

In both configurations, it is "possible" that using the "closed" ball 
shape or the "open" saddle shape it might be possible to travel from 
point A to point B faster than the presumptive linear distance.

In either configuration there is no way to ever reach the edge, you 
would either be unable to keep pace (open) or collapse just before you 
got there (closed).
```

Black holes are an example of a third possibility - closed universes
within a greater universe.   

While it doesn't fit current theory, one could have clusters of
galaxies forming a closed universe if the gravity was right - with
other clusters forming another closed universe.     We would need lots
more mass in those clusters for it to work, but it's a fun idea.

```

"charles hottel" <jghottel@yahoo.com> wrote in message 
news:bc342$43ad63f3$4f9c637$23393@DIALUPUSA.NET...
>I can understand that a two dimensional creature on the surface of an 
>expanding balloon could find distances increasing due to expansion of the 
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>dimension and manipulate according to the mathematical rules.

    There's this joke about a mathematician and a physicists attending a 
lecture. The lecturer goes on about terribly abstract concepts, mostly 
centered around a 37 dimensional figure. The physicist is frantically taking 
notes, scratching his head, and furrowing his brow, while the mathematician 
just sits calmly, arms crossed, occasionally nodding in agreement.

    At the end of the lecture, the physicists ask the mathematician a few 
questions, and soon realizes that the mathematician had no trouble 
understanding the concepts involved at all. He asks the mathematician how he 
was able to visualize a 37 dimensional space. The mathematician responded 
that he simply visualized an n-dimensional space, and lets n = 37.

    The trick that I've heard and use, is don't bother trying to visualize 
spaces of dimensions 5 or higher. You might be able to manage 4 dimensions, 
by thinking 3 spaces plus time (i.e. an "animation"), or you might be 
satisfied to limiting your visualizations to 3 dimensions or lower. Just try 
to understand the concepts in 0, 1, 2 and 3 dimensions, use induction to 
convince yourself that whatever concept you believe in will also be true in 
arbitrarily higher dimensions, and just leave it at that.

    The "shadow" trick you mention, I assume has to do with the idea that a 
3 dimensional object casts a 2 dimensional shadow onto a 2 dimensional 
surface, and so I suppose you could extend that to a n dimensional object 
casting an n-1 dimensional shadow onto an n-1 dimensional surface. I prefer 
to think in terms of "slices" than "shadows". Take a 3 dimensional object, 
and imagine taking a (3D) knife and cutting an infinitely thin slice out of 
it. You get a 2D object. If parts of the original object were "hollow", then 
you might not end up with a single continuous objects, put rather what 
appears to be several small 2D objects. For example, a slice through a donut 
might look like 2 circles that aren't attached to each other. When I try to 
visualize hypercubes, I usually visualize 3D slices of it. I usually don't 
get very far though.

    A 1D creature moving about a line might ask how it's possible for his 
universe to be without edges or centres? A 2D creature would say obviously 
that the "line" that the 1D creature is moving about must form a circle. But 
then the 2D creature moving about a surface will turn around and ask how 
it's possible for his universe to be without edges or centres. A 3D creature 
would say that this surface must be a sphere. Then the 3D creature moving 
about a volume will turn around and ask how it's possible for his universe 
to be without edges of centres. And this is where I stop visualizing, and 
just assume that this process can continue indefinitely onto to arbitrarily 
high dimensions.

    - Oliver
```

```

"James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca> wrote in message 
news:NKAsf.212161$Gd6.31575@pd7tw3no...
> I'll do a search on 'Mandelbrot set' and see if that turns up something 
> more specific. Actually I was hoping some bright spark would provide the 
> name of what I was looking at :-)

    What you mentioned, zooming in on seaweeds and seeing a self-similar 
structure which when itself zoomed in upon, reveals more self-similar 
structures, is called "fractal". The Mandelbrot set is one of the most 
famous fractals in Mathematics. Other popular ones include the Julia set and 
the Koch Snowflake.

    I don't know too many practical applications of fractals other than 
video games. They're used to generate realistic looking terrains, trees, 
galaxies (for space games), etc. They give a nice 
semi-structured-and-yet-semi-random look that shows up often in nature.

http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fractal

    - Oliver
```

"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:414p31F1coilcU3@individual.net...
>
>>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> which DON'T exist, have no such dimension.

    I think we won't find a section of our universe which is composed of 
space but not time. If such a thing were to exist, it would have to exist 
outside of our universe (where "outside" here means both outside of our 
space and outside of our time).

    For example, "after" our universe ceases to exist, a new universe could 
"come into existence", which had no time. There would just be matter 
arranged in space, and the matter would "never" move. "After" that, another 
universe could come into existence, this one being composed of both time and 
space. Words like "after", "never", etc. are not appopriate here, because 
they talk about timelessness, but hopefully the meaning is clear.

    Once Alistair brought it up, I realized that I am not aware of any 
scientific theory which supports the idea that space requires time, though I 
believe there are some which support the idea that time requires space. I 
vaguely recall a discussion that time is actually a "derived" concept, and 
the "base" concepts that make up the universe are space and velocity.

    - Oliver
```

…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
>    - Oliver
Space and time are related in very much the same way that energy and matter 
are. However my basis for sayng this is philosophical, rather than physical.

If you consider matter to be energy that is moving "slower" than the speed 
of light, and energy to be what matter converts to  when it approaches the 
speed of light (both gross but "useful" oversimplified distortions of 
physics), then you can imagine time arising out of space and space arising 
out of time. Relativity certainly implies that time is derived from 
movement, and movement certainly implies space.

It is all interesting speculation; for myself, I cannot conceive space and 
time as being anything other than interrelated.

(Gotta stop now as I have no more space or time :-))

Pete.

```
On Thu, 12 Jan 2006 12:44:37 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:

>>The universe would move from chaos to a more ordered state. That is 
>>observably not true. (See "Entropy")... If it was, my bed would make itself 
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>dis-assembled so that they would never harm anyone else.

An old SF story has a character who hopes his war wound isn't the
result of tripping on a tent stake.    The end of the story occurs
after him and his dad are sitting around, then they go to the hospital
where they meet his mother for the first time.

But if time really flowed backwards - who's to say that our memories
aren't also flowing backwards?   We still remember things from the
past - even if that's the direction we're moving.   Everything else is
based upon the past, and memory is just what was created from the
past, so our "now" would be the same as if memory was moving forward.

And since "now" is all we have, that's what we have to work with.
```

I'll grant that the language is difficult but the concept is simple. I
also have difficulty discussing religion without using capitals for the
start of God and Bible (although I deliverately did so at times during
this thread). Even as an atheist I am not consistent in my approach to
what I view as unimportant but deeply ingrained basic habits.

If all that exists is now does not make memories impossible and
predictions false.

Back to the one/two/three dimensional worlds. If a one dimensional
being perceives a dark point expand and break into two points which
move apart, move back together and then become a singular point, then
he may deduce the existence of a dark circle (and a second dimension)
having passed through his uni-dimensional world.

A two-dimensional creature may perceive that the dark point expands to
a dark circle before contracting back to a dark point again. That
creature may deduce the existence of a sphere  and a third dimension.

Because we have memories and can look forward to a happy retirement
does not mean that time exists. It may mean that we have a concept of
something that does may not exist.

Anyway, this is still academic and I look forward to the time when
academia settles the argument.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: religion

_(thread depth: 66)_

- **From:** "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com>
- **Date:** 2006-01-11T15:15:58+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Oi9xf.53080$km.35363@edtnps89>`
- **References:** `<dqcrp1hlglpf18tg9lmn3bbfqgofvo63st@4ax.com> <wXywf.80084$6K2.54082@edtnps90> <42hj02F1if80kU1@individual.net> <1136900927.606227.97620@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <dq0ium$dm3$1@reader2.panix.com> <1136988756.015434.172030@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com>`

```

"Alistair" <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote in message 
news:1136988756.015434.172030@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com...
>
> Back to the one/two/three dimensional worlds. If a one dimensional
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> creature may deduce the existence of a sphere  and a third dimension.

    I think most creatures of dimensionality N "perceive" an image in 
dimension N-1. Of course, the only samples I have to work with are 3 
dimensional creatures (namely life on earth), but it seems like a reasonable 
conclusion when you think of perception (or vision anyway) as being mostly 
the result of receiving rays (of light) that reflect against matter.

    For example, us, being 3D creatures, receive via each eye, a 2D image of 
the world around us (or directly in front of us, anyway). Most people have 
at most two eyes, so we can receive up to two images, and we can use the 
minor differences between each to try to infer some information about depth, 
but we don't "see" in 3D, in the sense that we can't see the backside of an 
object. If you're looking at a standard 6 sided dice, and you see the side 
marked "6", you will not be able to see the side marked "1" simultaneously.

    Similarly, imagine a 2D creature on a sheet of paper, and you draw a 
circle onto that sheet. The 2D creature cannot see the "other side" of that 
circle. He only sees the side facing him. So this 2D creature would probably 
receive a 1D image, which is a line. Perhaps the 2D creature will have 2 (or 
more) eyes, and can then infer some depth information about this line, and 
decide that what he sees is probably a circle, though he cannot see the 
backside, so he cannot know for sure until he walks around and checks it 
from all angles.

    Now, instead of a sheet of paper, imagine the 2D creature lives on a 
flat Euclidean, Cartesian, infinite 2D plane that travels through our 3D 
universe. The creature is only capable of moving along the plane, having no 
organs to allow it to travel along a 3rd axis. Let's say we push a sphere 
through the plane, very slowly, so that the creature has time to move around 
the sphere, and inspect it from all angles. At any one point in time, the 
creature is still receiving only a 1D image, but using the power of 
abstraction and his mind, he can determine that the 2D slice that he is 
seeing is a circle. As we push the sphere through the creature's plane of 
existing, it would seem that a tiny circle appears out of nowhere, grows for 
a while, and then shrinks again, and finally disappears again.

    If this were to happen to me, the first thing to jump into my mind is 
not "There must exist other dimensions" but rather "I guess matter is not 
conserved after all." Occam's Razor and all that.

    - Oliver
```

>
>docdwarf@panix.com wrote:

>> 'now' then memory and prediction would seem to be impossible.

[snip]

>If all that exists is now does not make memories impossible and
>predictions false.

Not 'false', likewise impossible.  Memory, by definition, is concerned 
with that which 'has been'; if all that exists is 'now' then 'has been' 
cannot exist and memory would seem to be impossible and likewise with 
prediction ('will be', by definition, does not exist because all that 
exists is 'now').

>
>Back to the one/two/three dimensional worlds.

'Flatland' (Abbott, 1884) covers this, I believe.

[snip]

>Because we have memories and can look forward to a happy retirement
>does not mean that time exists. It may mean that we have a concept of
>something that does may not exist.

I am not sure I agree or disagree with you here; I would need to know what 
you are using as criteria for the condition you postulate as 'existence'.

DD

- **Date:** 2006-01-11T14:17:36-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<43C567E0.68BEAABC@mts.net>`
- **References:** `<dqcrp1hlglpf18tg9lmn3bbfqgofvo63st@4ax.com> <1136900927.606227.97620@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <dq0ium$dm3$1@reader2.panix.com> <1136988756.015434.172030@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com> <dq3fdn$cn6$1@reader2.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> 
> Not 'false', likewise impossible.  Memory, by definition, is concerned
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> >


In fact, it's possible for time to pass for a person - a conscious
person - without it causing memory.  I have experienced this spooky and
counterintuitive state.  There's a drug - a derivative of a popular
tranquillizer - which prevents experience being bound into the brain
structure which contains our memory.  It's used after operations when
the patient is conscious and rational, but in pain or other serious
discomfort, so that the attending staff can communicate with him without
his having a sense of time passing and therefore not being oppressed by
the length of time he's hurting.  The result is that while you're there
and being intelligible, you don't remember it!  You might say that you
weren't thre at all!  Having experienced it I can tell you that not
being aware of pain (in this way) is well worth not remembering it!

Most of the problems encountered in the discussion of time come about
because language doesn't really allow for the distinction between
personal/subjective time - we perceive time passing - and objective time
- which is the perhaps non-existent succession of events outside our own
consciousness.  Thus: I can go into the past and change something which
(logically) ought to prevent the human race from happening.  That's to
say: I'm going to do it tomorrow, which means that it has already
happened, which means that I can't do it.  Subjectively, the situation
is yet to be; objectively, it either has or hasn't happened and what I
plan to do is irrelevant.  Whatever has happened, objectively, is
already fixed in time, regardless of how my subjective sense of time
orders it.  I don't think it's possible, even if time travel is, to
reverse one's own time flow vis a vis the objective time flow.

PL
```

A person?  I know I do that with wind... sorry, couldn't resist.

>Thus: I can go into the past and change something which
>(logically) ought to prevent the human race from happening.  That's to
>say: I'm going to do it tomorrow, which means that it has already
>happened, which means that I can't do it.

I believe this is a variant of the 'Grandfather Paradox': you 'goe back 
in time' and kills your grandfather before he can participate in the 
conception of your father.  Since your father was not conceived he did not 
exist to conceive you, since you were not conceived you did not exist... 
so how does that-which-did-not-exist kill your grandfather?

>Subjectively, the situation
>is yet to be; objectively, it either has or hasn't happened and what I
>plan to do is irrelevant.

As mentioned in another posting - if time can be made to 'run in reverse' 
then the universe becomes rigidly deterministic; nothing can be random.

DD

- **References:** `<dqcrp1hlglpf18tg9lmn3bbfqgofvo63st@4ax.com> <1136900927.606227.97620@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <dq0ium$dm3$1@reader2.panix.com> <1136988756.015434.172030@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com> <dq3fdn$cn6$1@reader2.panix.com> <43C567E0.68BEAABC@mts.net>`

```

"Peter Lacey" <lacey@mts.net> wrote in message 
news:43C567E0.68BEAABC@mts.net...
>
> In fact, it's possible for time to pass for a person - a conscious
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> being aware of pain (in this way) is well worth not remembering it!

    I've never heard of this before. Do you know the name of the drug? Do 
you know (e.g. were told) whether, during those moments of 
intelligibility-but-non-remembering, that you were aware of currently being 
in a state of pain?


>
> Most of the problems encountered in the discussion of time come about
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> orders it.

    From what I understand of the theory of relativity, an objective 
chronological ordering of events does not exist. Here, I'm defining event as 
some hypervolume in space and time. That is, an event could span a 
continuous amount of time, or just exist at a single point in time, and an 
event could span a continuous amount of space, or just exist at a single 
point in space. Since I assume space-time is 4 dimensional here, I used the 
term hypervolume.

    So given two events A and B, and two observers travelling at different 
velocities (preferrably both near the speed of light, but in different 
directions) with respect to the events, the two observers may disagree on 
whether event A occured before event B or the other way around.

> I don't think it's possible, even if time travel is, to
> reverse one's own time flow vis a vis the objective time flow.

    If time travel were possible, it might be possible for the traveller to 
continuously move to the instant "just before" the current instant, at the 
same rate at which that prior instant turned into the current one, so that 
the traveller perceives time to flow in the reverse direction.

    What this means depends on the mechanics of the time travel involved. 
Does the traveller simply project his consciousness around in time, so that 
as he travels backwards, his body gets younger? Or does he actually move the 
matter that makes up his body through time as well? If the latter, how is 
conservation of matter handled? Does an equivalent amount of matter a the 
destination-time of the journey get transported to the source-time? Or does 
some matter get obliterated at the destination-time (like in the Terminator 
system of time travel)? Etc.

    - Oliver
```

_(thread depth: 71)_

- **From:** "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com>
- **Date:** 2006-01-13T15:34:26+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6MPxf.79762$AP5.13644@edtnps84>`
- **References:** `<dqcrp1hlglpf18tg9lmn3bbfqgofvo63st@4ax.com> <1137101437.003563.306290@g44g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <dq6i5s$q92$1@reader2.panix.com> <1137105304.217221.214160@g44g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <dq6vl7$4js$1@reader2.panix.com>`

```
<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:dq6vl7$4js$1@reader2.panix.com...
>
> 'Imagine a universe where everything is cottage cheese.  Now if you take
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> Only 'now', Mr Maclean... no past or memories, no future or predictions.

    I can think of one way for a universe, without time but with memories, 
to exist depending on how relaxed you allow me to get with the definition of 
"memories"...

    Imagine an intelligent creator (not nescessarily so omnipotent that he 
can overcome logic) who chose to create a timeless universe which is an 
exact replica of the state of the universe at this very moment. There would 
be a planet whose inhabitants refer to as "Earth", there would exist humans 
sitting a computers, reading newsgroup postings, and some of them might be 
in the process of thinking. Others will be in the process of accessing their 
memories. Since this universe is timeless, the states will never change. 
I.e. the people who are thinking will "never" finish their thoughts. The 
people who are retrieving their memories will "never" actually retrieve 
them. The people who have "already" retrieved their memories will have those 
memories stuck in their minds "forever".

    One could argue that since this universe has no past, these things in 
the people's mind aren't memories at all. Or perhaps they are memories, but 
implanted by the creator. They are memories of events that "never happened" 
(or which happened in an alternative universe, one *with* time). Or one 
could argue that memories are nothing more than a byproduct of the state of 
the neurons in one's brain, and as long as the neurons are indeed in a valid 
state (as designed by our ID), then yes, memories exist.

    It's similar to considering someone in the process of speaking. The 
particles of air have certain values for momentum... That is, they "want" to 
move, if only time existed. Does this mean sound, defined as a fluctuation 
of pressure in a medium, exist? Is it still a wave, if it is frozen in time?

    If we choose to define sound as the perception of the fluctuations in 
pressure, rather than the fluctuations themselves, at what point does it 
become sound? As soon as the eardrum organ detects the change? During the 
period in which the ear drum is communicating with the brain? During the 
period in which neurons in the brain are communicating with each other about 
the signals from the eardrum? After the mind has already acknowledges the 
presence of this sound?

    - Oliver
```

```

"Alistair" <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote in message 
news:1136900927.606227.97620@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com...
>
> Suppose we consider a volume of space in which time does not exist. A
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>

    I think there are some very large conceptual problems with a volume of 
space without time surrounded by a volume of space *with* time. First, let's 
assume a discrete change from time-ness to timeless-ness, and a well defined 
border between the two regions of space. Any matter (e.g. the atoms that 
make up the matter that composes the organism) attempting to enter the 
region would be unable to do so, because to do so would change the contents 
of space at that point, and change implies the existence of time. So if that 
point of timeless space had matter, that matter could never be moved, and so 
you could never, for example, push that matter aside to insert your finger. 
If that point contaiend empty space, that empty space could never be filled. 
So what would happen if you tried to touch this empty space? Would you feel 
something pushing back to stop you? It seems to simply not make sense.

    A more "realistic" situation would be a continuous transition between 
time and timelessness. For example, well outside the boundaries of the 
timeless space region, time progresses "normally", but as we approach, time 
seems to progress more and more slowly, until at some point it the 
progression of time reaches zero, and we are in the timeless-region of 
space. Like Zeno's paradox, an organism trying to enter this region of 
timeless-ness could always get closer, but never actually reach that point.

    Isn't this what is thought to happen near a black hole anyway?

    - Oliver
```

> "Alistair" <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote in message 
…[35 more quoted lines elided]…
>    - Oliver
Three comments on a very lucid and thought provoking post... :-)

1. Space, by definition, is space, so 'empty space' is technically redundant 
and space is never 'empty' anyway...there are quantum fluctuations in the 
virtual and real fields.

2. I can't accept space without time; I can accept void or nothingness 
without time, but space is actually 'something'.

3. Xeno's paradox is demonstrably incorrect. Ask anyone who ever walked past 
a tortoise going in the same direction :-)

Pete.

PS yes, time is believed to stop at the event horizon of a BH. (There is 
still some debate about this...)


>
```

without time. Sorry, my sloppy use of English.

> assume a discrete change from time-ness to timeless-ness, and a well defined
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…

Aside from my comment above, there is the belief in science that some
parts of the universe are bubbles of space running at different rates
of time compared to our local space. I think that was one of the
explanations for our being able to see parts of the universe purporting
to be older than the universe or at least outside of our light cone
(sorry but we are reaching the limits of my knowledge).

As to borders around such bubbles of space, I think it unlikely that
there would be a rigid defined border, rather a gradient and that
probably compressed in the direction of travel. A gradient would be
passable. But that is academic as I am happy living in three
dimensions.

>
>     A more "realistic" situation would be a continuous transition between
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> timeless-ness could always get closer, but never actually reach that point.

I wonder what would happen to Zeno's paradox if time does not exist?
Perhaps the hare would race past the tortoise with ease?

>
>     Isn't this what is thought to happen near a black hole anyway?

I always thought that it was the observers' perception of the falling
object that it perpetually falls in to the hole but never quite gets to
the event horizon. I think that the object does fall in and at
increasing speed.


If time does not exist, could I argue that speed limits have no meaning
in law?

> 
>     - Oliver

I certainly can't close down your argument, Alastair. (It is a bit like the 
tree in the forest falling over... does it make a sound if no one is there 
to hear it? Logic tells us it must do, but it really depends what you mean 
by 'sound'. If you consider 'sound' to be the arrival of certain nerve 
impulses in a certain part of the brain, then it doesn't make a sound. If 
you consider 'sound' to be a wave motion of air, with an amplitude and 
frequency within a certain range, then it might indeed make a 'sound'. )

The fact that no-one's occupying your space and cannot therefore be aware of 
time passing, does not preclude it passing :-) It depends on what your 
definition of 'time' is. And that is the nub of the discussion.

I'm not persuaded by your argument, but I don't have enough authoritative 
data to refute it, so I just have to agree to disagree :-) I guess my 
concept of Time is largely based on intuition and faith... :-)

Pete.

```

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message 
news:rhf7s1lgprp7r3ginemsn3f7pi9s3picfc@4ax.com...
> On Mon, 09 Jan 2006 19:37:00 GMT, "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com>
> wrote:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> primacy over the other.

    Good question. I looked it up, and while I haven't finished reading all 
of these pages, these seem to address the issue.

This first page claims that both space and time are derived from a simpler 
concept which he calls "tise".
http://www.spaceandtimeareone.com/

<quote>
Space and time are great inventions of the mind to help it comprehend the 
world, at least in the scale range of our experience. But what could be the 
source in nature that led to the evolutionary development of these two 
tenacious and extremely useful perceptions? I have hypothesized that a 
single entity in nature , which, for convenient reference, I will call 
"tise," underlies both of these perceptions. Tise is all of what we perceive 
as space and time. Tise is comprised of infinitesimally small quanta, which 
I will call "tisons." I introduce the words "tise" (from TIme SpacE) and 
"tison" reluctantly, but necessarily, because tise is conceptually very 
different from the spacetime of modern physics.
</quote>

This page claims that both space and time are derived from the simpler 
concept of velocity.
http://www.reciprocalsystem.com/nlst/nlst06.htm

<quote>
An important consequence of the progression of space-time is that unit 
velocity, one unit of space per unit of time, is the condition of rest in 
the physical universe, the datum from which all activity begins. We are so 
accustomed to measuring from the mathematical zero that this concept of a 
finite velocity as the neutral condition will no doubt seem odd on first 
consideration, but it is not without precedent.
[...]
In this neutral condition, each unit of space is exactly like all other 
units of space and each unit of space is equivalent to a unit of time that 
is exactly like all other units of time. A unit of elapsed time, a unit 
movement in time, is equivalent to a unit movement in space, hence all 
locations in space-time are moving away from all other locations at unit 
velocity. Since space-time is motion, in the most general sense of that 
term, its measure is speed or, as this speed manifests itself in a spatial 
or temporal reference system, velocity. Just as we measure space in 
centimeters, or some similar unit, and time in seconds, we measure 
space-time in centimeters per second-in velocity terms. Unit velocity is not 
only the measure of the progression of space-time; it is the measure of 
space-time itself. Space-time is a motion: a progression. Aside from this 
ceaseless progression, a universe in the neutral condition would be one vast 
domain of perfect uniformity in which nothing ever happens and nothing could 
happen.
</quote>

    - Oliver
```

## Thread: New Year — 6 msgs

### New Year

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2006-01-01T12:28:56+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<41oihtF1fgaeeU1@individual.net>`

```
 
New Year's Eve passed off fairly quietly here in Tauranga.

I declined a couple of parties and opted instead for a quiet evening with a 
couple of friends at my place. (I'm definitely getting old :-))

The weather has been glorious (27 degrees C yesterday) and the evenings are 
balmy, so it was good for barbequing. We had a good assortment of local 
produce:  a couple of rock lobsters (caught by a friend), beef and pork 
fillets, lamb chops, spiced venison sausages (home made by another friend), 
mixed salads (provided by the guests), some excellent local wine, and fresh 
fruits of the forest. At midnight there was not the usual hooting of ships 
from the port (or, if there was, we didn't hear it) and a few fireworks left 
over from Guy Fawkes went off in a neighbours yard. We drank a toast to the 
new year, sang 'Auld Lang Syne' and that was it.

My house guest has now departed back to the U.K. and I need some time to 
recover from the hectic full-on schedule of sightseeing and having fun...:-)

Extended my holiday until the 9th so I can get some rest before heading back 
to Auckland and the hurly burly of the project. After the 9th, my posts here 
will be less frequent due to pressure of work, but in the interim I'd like 
to wish you all a Happy New Year and good things coming your way in 2006.

Pete
```

Thank you for the encouragement, Pradeep. (We all need a little sometimes... 
I'm glad you have enjoyed a glimpse into my world; I certainly enjoy the 
glimpses into everybody else's... :-)) These days I don't have much to say 
about COBOL (although I recently helped Oliver help someone who looked like 
he might have been a compatriot of yours, with COBOL and Java.)

 It's interesting to me that over the years, in thousands of posts here, 
most of which have at least had the word COBOL in them (if not always 
entirely on topic), like many others here, I have tried to assist people to 
solve problems, and 90% of them don't even say "Thanks". Free advice is 
worth the price and valued accordingly, I guess... Corporations, on the 
other hand, pay large sums of money for my time, experience, advice, and 
expertise, and are glad to get it. I guess there is a lesson there 
somewhere.

Not sure what it is, but it doesn't encourage me to help people, or post on 
COBOL. Besides, the OT posts are actually much more interesting for the most 
part than the on-topic ones :-).

I give extreme respect to people like Bill Klein and Richard Plinston (to 
name just two; no implicit devaluation of other frequent posters here) who 
demonstrate time and again, encyclopaedic knowledge of things COBOL and 
computers in general, and are happy to freely contribute their time here for 
the greater good. It makes me feel humble, and a bit churlish if I DON'T 
help when I obviously can... :-)

I'll continue to post here when I can, because I really treasure this forum 
as a bastion of free speech, and I hope it will go on long after I'm pushing 
up daisies.

Pete.

In article <421t1eF1gepb4U1@individual.net>,
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:

[snip]

> It's interesting to me that over the years, in thousands of posts here, 
>most of which have at least had the word COBOL in them (if not always 
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>part than the on-topic ones :-).

In keeping with the off-topic-ish nature, then, one might consider texts 
even older than COBOL... like the one which says '... and there is no new 
thing under the sun' (Eccl.I:9) or 'For gold in phisik is a cordial' (G. 
Chaucer, 'The Canterbury Tales', General Prologue, line 443)... and 
remember something I was told lo, those many years ago.

A physician I knew was doing his residency in the late 1950s; one of his 
co-residents did a regular bit of charity work (the medical equivalent of 
'pro bono') for a large family.  Before the beginning of each school-year 
this co-resident did simple physical exams for a family which had seven 
children; he'd just line them up and go through the routine ('head to the 
left and cough') so the kids' paperwork would be in order when their 
educations resumed.  Since the family was rather impoverished he was paid 
in cookies, a large plate of home-mades which the mother made.

One year he noticed that one of the girls had had a wound which had 
obviously been taken care of by A Professional; evidently the lass had 
fallen in a playground, picked up a laceration from a piece of broken 
glass and been sutured.  When he asked the child why she hadn't been taken 
to him for treatment she responded 'Oh no... Mommy says that we go to the 
Cookie Doctor *only* for physicals.'

He finished that round of examinations, accepted the plate of baked 
goods... and informed the mother that she would need to find someone else 
to tend to these matters.

DD

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol fo Unix

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2006-01-02T11:37:23-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1136230643.174032.95200@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<1136215961.283415.191000@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<43ae7eb7$0$27881$ed9e5944@text-readers.news.pipex.net> <bHJsf.36$Ic5.194629@news.sisna.com> <1136215961.283415.191000@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com>`

```
>  error while loading shared libraries: libcob.so.1: cannot open
> shared object file: No such file or directory

libcob.so.1 needs to be on your LD_LIBRARY_PATH. It should have been
copied into your /usr/local/lib directory and this should be in your
LD_LOAD_LIBRARY. If you did not install as root then it may not have
copied it there.

You may be able to simply add ~/??/open-cobol-0.31/libcob/.libs to your
library path and have it used from there:

LD_LIBRARY_PATH=${LD_LIBRARY_PATH}:open-cobol-0.31/libcob/.libs
export LD_LIBRARY_PATH
runprogram
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol fo Unix

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2006-01-04T23:40:35-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1136446835.108331.38980@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<1136443694.933677.181470@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<43ae7eb7$0$27881$ed9e5944@text-readers.news.pipex.net> <bHJsf.36$Ic5.194629@news.sisna.com> <1136215961.283415.191000@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com> <1136230643.174032.95200@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com> <1136443694.933677.181470@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`

```
> Thanks for the reply, I would appreciate if you give me some further
> information, as I've no idea how to add stuff to my library path.

> I installed it as root, and just did a reinstallation, as root, just to
> make sure, but it still runs the same error.

As root run:

updatedb

(this may take some time) which builds a database of all files on the
system.  When complete enter command (you don't need to be root):

locate libcob .so

This will list all the files with libcob.so in the path or name.

To find what your current library path is use the set command and the
grep command:

set | grep LD_LIBRARY_PATH

If the paths do not include the location of the libcob.so.1 then make a
shell script file:

vi run.sh       (or use your favourite editor)
----------------------------------------------------------
#!/bin/sh

LD_LIBRARY_PATH=${LD_LIBRARY_PATH}:path
export LD_LIBRARY_PATH

yourprogramname

-----------------------------------------------------------

where 'path' is where libcob.so.1 is
           'yourprogramname' is your program name

run this with:

sh run.sh
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol fo Unix

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** mwojcik@newsguy.com (Michael Wojcik)
- **Date:** 2006-01-06T15:45:27+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<dpm3an02l2s@news1.newsguy.com>`
- **References:** `<43ae7eb7$0$27881$ed9e5944@text-readers.news.pipex.net> <1136446835.108331.38980@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`

```

In article <1136446835.108331.38980@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>, "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> writes:
> Erez <moonbuzz@gmail.com>
> > Thanks for the reply, I would appreciate if you give me some further
> > information, as I've no idea how to add stuff to my library path.

That's a rather basic task - it's just a matter of changing the
value of an environment variable.  If you don't know how to do that,
you really need to read a primer for the shell you're using, and
probably one for Unix in general or the flavor you're running..

> > I installed it as root, and just did a reinstallation, as root, just to
> > make sure, but it still runs the same error.

Randomly stumbling around using the superuser account is a good way
to ruin your OS installation.

> To find what your current library path is use the set command and the
> grep command:
> 
> set | grep LD_LIBRARY_PATH

Not the best idiom, I think, since the "set" command is a builtin in
some, but not all, shells.  In some, notably csh and its derivatives,
it's a builtin that has a different function.  (While I don't
recommend csh, there are still people who use it.)

The "env" command is external, and should work in all shells, but even

   env | grep LD_LIBRARY_PATH

is unnecessarily complex.  I'd just echo the value of the variable:

   echo $LD_LIBRARY_PATH

That works in every shell I've used.

Note that while LD_LIBRARY_PATH contains the shared library path for
most contemporary Unix implementations, it's not universal.  Learning
the Unix (and shell) you're running is really the better plan.

(There are alternatives to the shell script solution Richard
recommends, too, but I suspect they'd just confuse Erez.)
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol fo Unix

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2006-01-06T17:01:49+00:00
